## Chapter: Chemical and Biological Reaction Engineering:

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will be discussing external mass-transfer resistance in chemical and biological reaction engineering. Mass transfer is the movement of molecules from one location to another, and it plays a crucial role in many chemical and biological processes. In the context of reaction engineering, mass transfer is important because it affects the rate at which reactants are brought together and converted into products. In this chapter, we will focus on external mass-transfer resistance, which refers to the resistance to mass transfer that occurs at the interface between the bulk fluid and the surface of a solid catalyst or biological material. We will explore the factors that influence external mass-transfer resistance and how it can be quantified and incorporated into reaction rate equations. Additionally, we will discuss strategies for minimizing external mass-transfer resistance in order to optimize reaction rates and improve process efficiency. Overall, this chapter will provide a comprehensive understanding of the role of external mass-transfer resistance in chemical and biological reaction engineering.


## Chapter 16: External Mass-Transfer Resistance:

### Section: 16.1 Gas-Liquid Reactions in Multiphase Systems:

In this section, we will discuss the basics of gas-liquid reactions in multiphase systems. Gas-liquid reactions are a common type of reaction in chemical and biological processes, where a gas and a liquid come into contact and react with each other. Examples of gas-liquid reactions include the absorption of gases into liquids, such as the removal of carbon dioxide from flue gas in a power plant, and the production of chemicals through gas-liquid reactions, such as the production of ethanol through the fermentation of sugars.

Gas-liquid reactions in multiphase systems are characterized by the presence of two phases: a gas phase and a liquid phase. The gas phase is typically composed of one or more gases, while the liquid phase can be a pure liquid or a mixture of liquids. The interface between the two phases is where the reaction takes place, and it is also where external mass-transfer resistance occurs.

#### 16.1a Basics of Gas-Liquid Reactions

In order for a gas-liquid reaction to occur, the gas molecules must first dissolve into the liquid phase. This process is known as absorption or dissolution. The rate of absorption is influenced by several factors, including the solubility of the gas in the liquid, the surface area of the interface, and the concentration gradient between the gas and liquid phases.

Once the gas molecules have dissolved into the liquid, they can react with the molecules in the liquid phase. This reaction can be either homogeneous, where the reactants and products are in the same phase, or heterogeneous, where the reactants and products are in different phases. The rate of the reaction is determined by the kinetics of the reaction and the concentration of the reactants.

In gas-liquid reactions, external mass-transfer resistance occurs at the interface between the gas and liquid phases. This resistance is caused by the physical barrier between the two phases, which hinders the movement of molecules from one phase to another. The resistance can be quantified using the Sherwood number, which is a dimensionless number that relates the mass-transfer coefficient to the diffusivity of the gas in the liquid.

To incorporate external mass-transfer resistance into reaction rate equations, the Sherwood number can be used to calculate the mass-transfer coefficient, which is then used to determine the rate of absorption and reaction. Strategies for minimizing external mass-transfer resistance include increasing the surface area of the interface, increasing the agitation of the system, and using catalysts or enzymes to facilitate the reaction.

In conclusion, gas-liquid reactions in multiphase systems are important in many chemical and biological processes, and external mass-transfer resistance plays a crucial role in determining the rate of these reactions. Understanding the basics of gas-liquid reactions and how to minimize external mass-transfer resistance is essential for optimizing reaction rates and improving process efficiency. 


## Chapter 16: External Mass-Transfer Resistance:

### Section: 16.1 Gas-Liquid Reactions in Multiphase Systems:

In this section, we will discuss the basics of gas-liquid reactions in multiphase systems. Gas-liquid reactions are a common type of reaction in chemical and biological processes, where a gas and a liquid come into contact and react with each other. Examples of gas-liquid reactions include the absorption of gases into liquids, such as the removal of carbon dioxide from flue gas in a power plant, and the production of chemicals through gas-liquid reactions, such as the production of ethanol through the fermentation of sugars.

Gas-liquid reactions in multiphase systems are characterized by the presence of two phases: a gas phase and a liquid phase. The gas phase is typically composed of one or more gases, while the liquid phase can be a pure liquid or a mixture of liquids. The interface between the two phases is where the reaction takes place, and it is also where external mass-transfer resistance occurs.

#### 16.1a Basics of Gas-Liquid Reactions

In order for a gas-liquid reaction to occur, the gas molecules must first dissolve into the liquid phase. This process is known as absorption or dissolution. The rate of absorption is influenced by several factors, including the solubility of the gas in the liquid, the surface area of the interface, and the concentration gradient between the gas and liquid phases.

Once the gas molecules have dissolved into the liquid, they can react with the molecules in the liquid phase. This reaction can be either homogeneous, where the reactants and products are in the same phase, or heterogeneous, where the reactants and products are in different phases. The rate of the reaction is determined by the kinetics of the reaction and the concentration of the reactants.

In gas-liquid reactions, external mass-transfer resistance occurs at the interface between the gas and liquid phases. This resistance is caused by the physical barrier between the two phases, which hinders the transfer of mass between them. The resistance can be affected by various factors, including the properties of the gas and liquid, the geometry of the system, and the flow conditions.

#### 16.1b Factors Affecting Gas-Liquid Reactions

There are several factors that can affect the rate of gas-liquid reactions in multiphase systems. These factors can be divided into two categories: those that affect the absorption of gas into the liquid phase, and those that affect the reaction itself.

##### Absorption Factors

The rate of absorption of gas into the liquid phase is influenced by the following factors:

- Solubility of the gas in the liquid: This is the most important factor affecting the absorption rate. The higher the solubility of the gas in the liquid, the faster the absorption will occur.
- Surface area of the interface: A larger surface area allows for more gas molecules to come into contact with the liquid, increasing the rate of absorption.
- Concentration gradient between the gas and liquid phases: A larger concentration gradient results in a higher driving force for the gas to dissolve into the liquid.

##### Reaction Factors

The rate of the reaction itself is influenced by the following factors:

- Kinetics of the reaction: The rate of the reaction is determined by the kinetics of the specific reaction taking place.
- Concentration of the reactants: A higher concentration of reactants will result in a faster reaction rate.

Understanding these factors is crucial in designing and optimizing gas-liquid reactions in multiphase systems. By controlling these factors, engineers can improve the efficiency and effectiveness of these reactions in various industrial processes.


## Chapter 16: External Mass-Transfer Resistance:

### Section: 16.1 Gas-Liquid Reactions in Multiphase Systems:

In this section, we will discuss the basics of gas-liquid reactions in multiphase systems. Gas-liquid reactions are a common type of reaction in chemical and biological processes, where a gas and a liquid come into contact and react with each other. Examples of gas-liquid reactions include the absorption of gases into liquids, such as the removal of carbon dioxide from flue gas in a power plant, and the production of chemicals through gas-liquid reactions, such as the production of ethanol through the fermentation of sugars.

Gas-liquid reactions in multiphase systems are characterized by the presence of two phases: a gas phase and a liquid phase. The gas phase is typically composed of one or more gases, while the liquid phase can be a pure liquid or a mixture of liquids. The interface between the two phases is where the reaction takes place, and it is also where external mass-transfer resistance occurs.

#### 16.1a Basics of Gas-Liquid Reactions

In order for a gas-liquid reaction to occur, the gas molecules must first dissolve into the liquid phase. This process is known as absorption or dissolution. The rate of absorption is influenced by several factors, including the solubility of the gas in the liquid, the surface area of the interface, and the concentration gradient between the gas and liquid phases.

Once the gas molecules have dissolved into the liquid, they can react with the molecules in the liquid phase. This reaction can be either homogeneous, where the reactants and products are in the same phase, or heterogeneous, where the reactants and products are in different phases. The rate of the reaction is determined by the kinetics of the reaction and the concentration of the reactants.

In gas-liquid reactions, external mass-transfer resistance occurs at the interface between the gas and liquid phases. This resistance is caused by the physical barrier between the two phases, which hinders the transfer of mass between them. This resistance can be due to several factors, such as the thickness of the liquid film, the diffusivity of the gas in the liquid, and the velocity of the gas and liquid phases.

#### 16.1b Types of External Mass-Transfer Resistance

There are two main types of external mass-transfer resistance: film resistance and penetration resistance. Film resistance occurs when the gas must pass through a liquid film before reaching the interface, while penetration resistance occurs when the gas must penetrate through a layer of stagnant liquid before reaching the interface. Both types of resistance can significantly affect the rate of gas-liquid reactions and must be taken into account when designing and optimizing these processes.

#### 16.1c Case Studies in External Mass-Transfer Resistance

To further understand the impact of external mass-transfer resistance on gas-liquid reactions, let's look at some case studies. In the first case study, we will examine the removal of carbon dioxide from flue gas in a power plant. The gas-liquid reaction in this case is the absorption of carbon dioxide into a liquid solvent. The rate of absorption is affected by the thickness of the liquid film and the diffusivity of carbon dioxide in the solvent. By optimizing these factors, the efficiency of carbon dioxide removal can be improved.

In the second case study, we will explore the production of ethanol through the fermentation of sugars. In this gas-liquid reaction, the rate of absorption is influenced by the concentration gradient between the gas and liquid phases, as well as the velocity of the gas and liquid phases. By controlling these factors, the yield of ethanol can be increased.

These case studies demonstrate the importance of considering external mass-transfer resistance in gas-liquid reactions and how it can be manipulated to improve the efficiency and yield of these processes. In the next section, we will delve deeper into the calculations and equations used to quantify external mass-transfer resistance in gas-liquid reactions. 


## Chapter 16: External Mass-Transfer Resistance:

### Section: 16.2 Oxygen Transfer in Fermentors:

In this section, we will discuss the basics of oxygen transfer in fermentors. Fermentors are vessels used in the production of various chemicals and pharmaceuticals through biological processes. These processes involve the use of microorganisms, such as bacteria or yeast, to convert raw materials into desired products. Oxygen transfer is a crucial aspect of these processes, as it is necessary for the growth and metabolism of the microorganisms.

#### 16.2a Basics of Oxygen Transfer

Oxygen transfer in fermentors occurs through the process of gas-liquid absorption. Oxygen gas from the atmosphere dissolves into the liquid medium in the fermentor, where it is then available for the microorganisms to use. The rate of oxygen transfer is influenced by several factors, including the solubility of oxygen in the liquid medium, the surface area of the gas-liquid interface, and the concentration gradient between the gas and liquid phases.

Once oxygen is dissolved into the liquid medium, it can be utilized by the microorganisms for their metabolic processes. This can lead to the production of desired products, such as ethanol in the fermentation of sugars. The rate of oxygen utilization is determined by the kinetics of the metabolic reactions and the concentration of oxygen in the liquid medium.

In fermentors, external mass-transfer resistance occurs at the gas-liquid interface. This resistance is caused by the physical barriers that hinder the transfer of oxygen from the gas phase to the liquid phase. These barriers can include the thickness of the gas-liquid boundary layer, the presence of bubbles or foam, and the agitation of the liquid medium.

To optimize oxygen transfer in fermentors, it is important to consider the design and operating conditions of the vessel. Increasing the surface area of the gas-liquid interface, such as through the use of aeration devices, can improve oxygen transfer. Additionally, controlling the agitation and foam formation can also help to reduce external mass-transfer resistance. Overall, understanding the basics of oxygen transfer in fermentors is crucial for efficient and successful biological processes.
